Standing Amidst ‚ÄúPower‚Äù
April 26, 2012

Tags: Ayanna Pressley, Boston, Boston Magazine, Deval Patrick, LeadBoston, Massachusetts, Matthew Stewart, media, power, privilege, race, racial justice, Simmons College, Stand Against Racism, The Power Complex, white men, whiteness, women, ywca, YWCA Boston 



by Matthew Stewart
YWCA Boston
Matthew Stewart asks, What‚Äôs missing from this Boston Magazine cover? Click to enlarge.
In the same month that YWCA Boston is sponsoring the city‚Äôs third annual Stand Against Racism, Boston Magazine has distributed its April issue entitled ‚ÄúPOWER‚Äù with the main story featuring ‚ÄúThe 50 People Who Run This Town.‚Äù The magazine‚Äôs cover proudly presents 10 leaders from its ‚Äúpower list,‚Äù nearly all of whom are white (and six of whom are white men).
A casual glance of the magazine cover in a local store renders yet another cognitive coupling of whiteness and power, reinforcing a cultural script that defines whiteness as naturally superior. Ironically titled ‚ÄúThe Power Complex,‚Äù the article appears to deconstruct itself ‚Äî as the title suggests a correlation between whiteness and not only power complexes but power as ‚Äúsheer force of will.‚Äù Of the 50 people ranked as those who ‚Äúrun this town,‚Äù at least 40 are white; the major exceptions being Boston City Councilor Ayanna Pressley and Massachusetts Governor Deval Patrick.
Last year, hundreds of people took a stand against racism by gathering and forming a human chain across the Summer and Congress street bridges in downtown Boston. Read the full article by the Boston Globe by clicking the image.
Perhaps what is most harmful about this cover image is that it normalizes an image of ‚ÄúPOWER‚Äù with an image of whiteness; more specifically, an image of whiteness that remains an invisible standard left unquestioned. By emphasizing ‚Äútraditional power‚Äù as localized in a specific intersection of race, class, and gender, the article not only homogenizes power as a largely white, affluent, male enterprise but also ignores the very powerful innovations being made by people and diverse organizations in Boston who do not fit this category.
The article introduces Boston‚Äôs ‚Äúpower list‚Äù by describing these 50 leaders as ‚Äúthe players charting new directions for our city.‚Äù However, the framing of such leaders as predominantly white only seems to reiterate the past and Boston‚Äôs recurring legacy of racism. It is important to challenge the images precisely because something a commonly picked up as a magazine reproduces cultural imaginaries of race, class, and gender-based on oppressive assumptions.
YWCA Boston‚Äôs Stand Against Racism is a direct response to such social complicities. In addition to its ongoing facilitations of community dialogues on race and ethnicity, YWCA Boston has innovative programming that is addressing these issues for both the present and future. Its LeadBoston program brings together local business executives for experiential learning about key disparities in Boston and its Youth Initiative is fostering a progressive community of local high school youth who are already shaping Boston‚Äôs future. YWCA Boston has recruited nearly 100 participating sites for the 2012 Stand Against Racism, and its continuing efforts insure that standing against racism and other intersecting systems of oppression will be its overarching primary goal every day of the year.
More information about YWCA Boston and its programming can be found at www.ywcaboston.org.
Matthew Stewart is the community outreach intern at YW Boston and is a Gender/Cultural Studies graduate student at Simmons College. He is coordinating Stand Against Racism events at Simmons.

This post is part of the YWCA Stand Against Racism blog carnival on issues of race, justice and diversity. We invite you to join the dialogue! Post your comment below, share your story and follow the conversation on Twitter with the hashtag #StandAgainstRacism.
